  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c29745e7-e0c3-419d-be81-247d46fe8778'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14776222519261053390">Alternate Cover Edition ISBN 0805090037 (ISBN13: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40239818-bring-up-the-bodies' rel='nofollow noopener'>9780805090031</a>)<br/><br/>The sequel to Hilary Mantel's international bestseller and Man Booker Prize winner Wolf Hall explores one of the most mystifying and frightening episodes in English history: the downfall of Anne Boleyn.<br/>...<br/>Though he battled for years to marry her, Henry VIII has become disenchanted with the audacious Anne Boleyn</span>
  <span id="freeText14776222519261053390" style="display:none">Alternate Cover Edition ISBN 0805090037 (ISBN13: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40239818-bring-up-the-bodies" rel="nofollow noopener">9780805090031</a>)<br /><br />The sequel to Hilary Mantel's international bestseller and Man Booker Prize winner Wolf Hall explores one of the most mystifying and frightening episodes in English history: the downfall of Anne Boleyn.<br />...<br />Though he battled for years to marry her, Henry VIII has become disenchanted with the audacious Anne Boleyn. She has failed to give him a son, and her sharp intelligence and strong will have alienated his old friends and the noble families of England.<br /><br />When the discarded Katherine, Henry's first wife, dies in exile from the court, Anne stands starkly exposed, the focus of gossip and malice, setting in motion a dramatic trial of the queen and her suitors for adultery and treason. <br /><br />At a word from Henry, Thomas Cromwell is ready to bring her down. Over a few terrifying weeks, Anne is ensnared in a web of conspiracy, while the demure Jane Seymour stands waiting her turn for the poisoned wedding ring. But Anne and her powerful family will not yield without a ferocious struggle. To defeat the Boleyns, Cromwell must ally himself with his enemies. What price will he pay for Annie's head? <br />(front flap)</span>

            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>    </div>
</div>

<div id="review_332126853" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/332126853" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/166376-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345482402p2/166376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/332126853?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/166376-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13644297-bring-up-the-bodies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/166376-david?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer332126853" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10722814978920098184">The normally flinty James Wood recently wrote what can only be characterized as an extended mash note to Hilary Mantel in the New Yorker, based on this book and its predecessor, Wolf Hall. I can only concur, and add a few observations of my own.<br/><br/>How good is this book? It's so good that<br/>(i) I am trying to ration myself to only 50 pages a day, to spin out the experience of reading it just that little bit longer<br/>(ii) I am failing miserably in objective (i) above, because I am an undisciplined wretch, </span>
  <span id="freeText10722814978920098184" style="display:none">The normally flinty James Wood recently wrote what can only be characterized as an extended mash note to Hilary Mantel in the New Yorker, based on this book and its predecessor, Wolf Hall. I can only concur, and add a few observations of my own.<br /><br />How good is this book? It's so good that<br />(i) I am trying to ration myself to only 50 pages a day, to spin out the experience of reading it just that little bit longer<br />(ii) I am failing miserably in objective (i) above, because I am an undisciplined wretch, completely lacking in self-control, and I just can't help myself<br />(iii) I call people up on the other side of the Atlantic, just to read them choice sentences<br />(iv) I feel impelled to share a few of those sentences with you<br /><br />Thomas Cromwell is attempting to sway the deposed queen Katherine of Aragon and says something to incite her displeasure:<br /><br />"There is a pause, while she turns the great pages of her volume of rage, and puts her finger on just the right word"<br /><br />of one of Anne Boleyn's ladies-in-waiting:<br /><br />"If someone said to Lady Rochford, 'It's raining,' she would turn it into a conspiracy; as she passed the news on, she would make it sound somehow indecent, unlikely, but sadly true."<br /><br />I'm not sure if James Wood actually went as far as to say that he would be happy to read Hilary Mantel's grocery list. But, based on the quality of the writing in the "Wolf Hall" books, I would.<br />You wouldn't think it possible to tell the story of the Tudors and make it fresh. But Mantel succeeds once again, brilliantly.<br /><br />Added on edit after finishing:<br /><br />The last 50 pages of this are frightening, and frighteningly good. James Wood offers far more insight into what he calls Mantel's "novelistic intelligence", also on the topic of "authenticity" (where he makes a compelling case that fiction can offer a kind of authenticity that actually surpasses historical accuracy) than I ever could (though I found myself agreeing with everything he wrote, and the examples he cites are the same ones I would cite), so here is a link to his review -- I think it is accessible even in you don't have a New Yorker subscription.<br /><br /><a href="http://www.newyorker.com/arts/critics/books/2012/05/07/120507crbo_books_wood?currentPage=all" rel="nofollow noopener"> Wood on Mantel </a><br /><br />And finally, a note from Hilary Mantel, promising future delights.<br /><br />".... Thomas Cromwell, who is still in need of attention from biographers. Meanwhile, Mr Secretary remains sleek, plump and densely inaccessible, like a choice plum in a Christmas pie; but I hope to continue my efforts to dig him out".<br /><br />Bonne continuation, Mme Mantel, bonne continuation !!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10722814978920098184" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1069312663" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1069312663" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1069312663?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007315093" href="/book/show/13393094-bring-up-the-bodies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=man-booker-prize-winners">man-booker-prize-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=4-star-reads">4-star-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1069312663" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13446744980523608098">Mantel is such an excellent writer; her prose is eloquent and artistic, beautiful even.. Few writers have such skill. She uses every grammatical tool at her disposal to give her novel a strong individual sense of stylistic flair. And that’s just the surface level of her sentences; she also uses metaphor and constant allusions to take it to another level entirely. <br/><br/>For example, my favourite passage in the book: <br/><br/><i>“He looks around at his guests. All are prepared. A Latin grace; English would be his c</i></span>
  <span id="freeText13446744980523608098" style="display:none">Mantel is such an excellent writer; her prose is eloquent and artistic, beautiful even.. Few writers have such skill. She uses every grammatical tool at her disposal to give her novel a strong individual sense of stylistic flair. And that’s just the surface level of her sentences; she also uses metaphor and constant allusions to take it to another level entirely. <br><br>For example, my favourite passage in the book: <br><br><i>“He looks around at his guests. All are prepared. A Latin grace; English would be his choice, but he will suit his company. Who cross themselves ostentatiously, in papist style. Who look at him, expectant. He shouts for the waiters. The doors burst open. Sweating men heave the platters to the table. It seems the meat is fresh, in fact not slaughtered yet. It is just a minor breach of etiquette. The company must sit and salivate. The Boleyns are laid at his hand to be carved.”</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1498988926i/23179331._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1498988926i/23179330._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="500" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This is so much stronger that its predecessor <i> Wolf Hall. </i> There is a stronger narrative drive and Cromwell has more momentum and enthusiasm for achieving his ambitions. He is also getting used to his power and his influence, testing them to see how far they can reach. A risky game, but we all know what came of it in the end. He is a very intelligent man, able to root out evidence for the king when there is none to be found. The Boleyn family, though politically smart, were vastly out matched by the cunning of Cromwell. When the king wanted them out, Cromwell didn’t have to work too hard to achieve it. <br><br>Mantel has improved on her style so much here. <i> Wolf Hall</i> was exceptionally good, but it did have many flaws. It was a hard book to read. My main problem with it was trying to discern who the “he” in question within the writing. For example Cromwell was referred to “he” whist talking to another “he” about a pair of “he’s” they were feared were scheming against them. Uncomfortable stuff, though she seems to have listened to the criticism her first book received and the result is a much stronger piece of writing. <br><br><b> So what’s it all about?</b><br><br>Boredom. Boredom and anger. Henry VIII’s new wife Ann Boleyn has failed to give him a son, such a terrible thing. The entire situation is ridiculously ironic considering the one child she did give him, the future Elizabeth I, would become a much better monarch than he could ever be. The fat fool didn’t need a son! But his silly little masculine ego demanded one. Sure you could talk about the politics involved in having a male offspring, but, again, just look at what Elizabeth did by herself. She had no direct heir. When she died she gave the throne to the King of Scotland. Enough said. <br><br>Cromwell has a large task on his hands, but he was more than capable of carving up the Boleyns. The only real complication was he had to do it within the limitations of the law. The king can’t be above his own mandates, and he has to be able to justify his brutal actions even if the entire world new he was full of rubbish and would bend the system to his own ends and act all innocent about it. Then blame his councillors’ years later for his own decisions. Cromwell, naturally, goes to work. But his day of reckoning draws very near. <br><br>I can’t wait to read the final book in this series. It’s obvious how it will all end: the chopping block. It will be interesting to see how Mantel handles the fall of Cromwell. I also wonder if she will win the man booker prize again. I think she’s great, but I don’t think she deserves it again. The prize is for innovating, high quality writing. But her writing is no longer innovative. It’s a regurgitation of what she has already done (not a bad thing) but I don’t think she should win the prize multiple times for doing the same things. Still, I'm excited for the third book.<br><br>__________________________________<br><br>You can connect with me on social media via <a href="https://linktr.ee/sean.ultra_vegan1" rel="nofollow noopener">My Linktree</a>.<br>__________________________________</span>
  <a data-text-id="13446744980523608098" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3375883160" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3893898234682411510">Booker Prize 2012<br/>Costa Book Award 2012<br/>Women’s Prize for Fiction nominee 2013<br/><br/>Brilliant! A masterful piece of literature! I loved WOLF HALL, the first book in the Thomas Cromwell series, but I have to say I enjoyed BRING UP THE BODIES even more If that is even possible. There is no lack of excitement in book #2. Henry VIII asks Cromwell to get rid of Anne Boleyn as she hasn’t given him a son. The King wants to make way for another, Jane Seymour, that has caught his eye. His first wife, Katherine, </span>
  <span id="freeText3893898234682411510" style="display:none">Booker Prize 2012<br />Costa Book Award 2012<br />Women’s Prize for Fiction nominee 2013<br /><br />Brilliant! A masterful piece of literature! I loved WOLF HALL, the first book in the Thomas Cromwell series, but I have to say I enjoyed BRING UP THE BODIES even more If that is even possible. There is no lack of excitement in book #2. Henry VIII asks Cromwell to get rid of Anne Boleyn as she hasn’t given him a son. The King wants to make way for another, Jane Seymour, that has caught his eye. His first wife, Katherine, has died so all is well to move on to wife #3 once a plan is put together for Queen Anne’s demise. <br /><br />BRING UP THE BODIES is character-driven and thoroughly engaging. Be ready to live in the Tudor world, their real world. Expect some of the best dialogue you have ever set your eyes on or listened to. You will be so entertained with the court intrigue, accusations of adultery and treason, the power yielded, and finally death and execution.<br /><br />If you haven’t done so, do look for the PBS production, WOLF HALL, which is a combination of the first two books of the trilogy. I just started listening to book #3 THE MIRROR AND THE LIGHT and will be looking forward to the upcoming PBS production of the last of the trilogy.<br /><br />A phenomenal 5 out of 5 stars<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1270402625" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1270402625" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1270402625?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13722528-bring-up-the-bodies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=books-of-the-year-2012">books-of-the-year-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1270402625" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer534116083807057769"><blockquote>
  <b> His whole career has been an education in hypocrisy. Eyes that once skewered him now kindle with simulated regard. Hands that would like to knock his hat off now reach out to take his hand, sometimes in a crushing grip. He has spun his enemies to face him, to join him: as in a dance. He means to spin them away again, so they look down the long cold vista of their years: so they feel the wind, the wind of exposed places, that cuts to the bone: so they bed down in ruins, and wake up cold.</b>
</blockquote>Be c</span>
  <span id="freeText534116083807057769" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b> His whole career has been an education in hypocrisy. Eyes that once skewered him now kindle with simulated regard. Hands that would like to knock his hat off now reach out to take his hand, sometimes in a crushing grip. He has spun his enemies to face him, to join him: as in a dance. He means to spin them away again, so they look down the long cold vista of their years: so they feel the wind, the wind of exposed places, that cuts to the bone: so they bed down in ruins, and wake up cold.</b>
</blockquote>Be careful what you wish for. Henry VIII was pining for the younger-than-his-current-wife Anne Boleyn. After getting his heart’s desire, which required him to take on the Catholic Church, one might imagine him speaking to Thomas Cromwell as Ollie might have said to Laurel, “<a href="http://www.stanlaurelandoliverhardy.com/nicemess.htm" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Well, here’s another nice mess you’ve gotten me into!</a>” nicely demonstrating an inability to accept any responsibility for his own actions. Of course, AB had gotten her heart’s desire as well, a nifty crown, plenty of staff, and she gets to headline at the palace. But pride, and not popping out a male heir, goeth before the fall, and well, the girl should have known. I mean H8 was not exactly a model hubby to his first wife. Why would she think he’d be any more loyal to her? Time for the head of household to summon Mister Fixit.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1432196140i/14909020.jpg" width="183" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1379751258i/133208.jpg" width="210" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Rafe Sadler</b> and <b>Stephen Gardiner</b> <br><br>Looking for advice on ridding yourself of unwanted household pests? Running low on funds for your comfortable lifestyle? Need the occasional hard thump to the torso to get the old ticker restarted? Need to re-direct your reproductive efforts towards a more masculine outcome? Need to fend off potential assaults by enemies foreign and domestic? Why, call Mister Fixit (Yes, yes, I know there were no phones in 16th Century England, so <i>summon</i> Mr. Fixit. OK? Happy now? Jeez, some people). Thomas Cromwell, a man of modest origins who had risen to the highest position in the land, that did not absolutely require aristocratic genes, had already demonstrated a penchant for getting things done, by whatever means necessary. And so continues the tale, in book 2 of Hilary Mantel’s trilogy about Tudor England.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1432207047i/14909542._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Hilary Mantel</b><br><br>The end of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1230625104" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolf Hall</a> (You read <i>Wolf Hall</i>, right? If you haven’t, stop reading this now, and go get a copy. Read that and when you are done, feel free to return. What are you waiting for? Go! Scat!) was H8’s marriage to AB. The quest had come to the desired conclusion, and now they’re gonna party like it’s 1533. Not only had H8 succeeded in flipping the bird (a falcon in this case – see the badges below) to the RC, but he was engaged in swiping their stuff as well. <i>Pope? We doan need no steenking Pope</i>. Cromwell was the guy who had done most of the fixing. So everything should be fine now, right? Not so fast.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1432193616i/14908876.jpg" width="206" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1432194044i/14908882.jpg" width="190" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Dueling Badges – Anne Boleyn’s and Catherine of Aragon’s</b> - in case any are needed<br><br>AB is getting very full of herself but not, unfortunately full of a male heir, and there are younger ladies-in-waiting, you know, waiting. H8 has an eye problem. It wanders uncontrollably, in this instance to young, demure Jane Seymour. Of course there is the pesky business of clearing that obstruction from the royal path, and Mister Fixit is called in (sorry, summoned) to make it go away. Luckily for him he has his fingers in many administrative pies (you washed those fingers before inserting, right?) and is not shy about using his inside knowledge to achieve his boss’s goals. Cromwell also has an excellent network of spies (<a href="https://gameofthrones.fandom.com/wiki/Little_birds" rel="nofollow noopener">little birds?</a>) sprinkled throughout the realm. Combine the two, make much of what was probably idle gossip, add a dollop or three of spite and voila. For good measure, TC takes particular pleasure in focusing his skills on those who had done dirt to his mentor, Cardinal Wolsey, ticking off each one as they succumb to his devilry. (Like a certain Stark lass ticking off her list of future targets at bedtime) <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1432196795i/14909053._SY540_.jpg" width="217" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1432196795i/14909054._SY540_.jpg" width="175" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>The once and future – Catherine of Aragon and Jane Seymour</b><br><br>Was AB guilty of the crimes of which she was accused? Probably not. But as long as the folks in charge can get the people with weapons to do their bidding it does not much matter. There is no law, really, only power. Legal processes are often mere window dressing to the underlying exercise of big fish eating smaller fish, and sometimes spitting them out. The fiction of legality keeps the mass of smaller fish from chomping their much larger tormenters to bits. Sort of like <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2015/02/28/nyregion/exxon-mobil-settles-with-new-jersey-over-environmental-damage.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">now</a>. See, people? It’s all perfectly legal.<br><br><i>Bring Up the Bodies</i> is a masterful achievement, showing, step-by-step, how dark aims are orchestrated and achieved. In laying this out, Hilary Mantel also offers us a look at how the reins of power can be abused by the unscrupulous, and Thomas Cromwell is shown in his full unscrupulousness in this volume. He was gonna get these guys and when he saw his chance, he took it. Where <i>Wolf Hall</i> presented a more removed Cromwell, <i>Bring Up the Bodies</i> shows us Cromwell as more than a fixer, more than a technocrat. We get to see him as a monster, despite his supposed desire to make England more equitable for working people. <br><br>H8 is shown much more as a spoiled psycho-child in this volume. Whatever his intelligence, whatever his accomplishments, what we see of Henry here is primarily his boorishness, his childishness. I want what I want and I do not care who gets hurt, or even killed, so I can have it. I was reminded of the great Twilight Zone episode <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HoDPSTme3zU" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> It’s a Good Life</a>.<br><br>Mantel won a second Booker prize for this one, and it was well deserved. Not only do we get a very human look at a key period in Western history, but are blessed with Mantel’s amazing wit as manifested by her characters, and consideration of issues that transcend history, as well as a compelling episode of Survival: Tudor. It is an easier read than the first book, more engaging, if that is possible. If you have not seen the miniseries made from the combined volumes you really must. Hilary Mantel has brought out her best in <i>Bring Up the Bodies</i>, using her genius for historical fiction to make the old seem new again. You won’t lose your head if you don’t read this book, but you probably should.<br><br>Review posted – 5/22/15<br>Re-posted 5/24/19<br><br>Publication date – 5/8/2012<br><br>The final volume in the series, <i>The Mirror and the Light</i>, is due for release March 2020, according to the latest intel. <br><br>=============================<b>EXTRA STUFF</b><br><br>My review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1230625104" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolf Hall</a><br><br>Links to the author’s <a href="http://hilary-mantel.com/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">personal</a>, <a href="https://twitter.com/hilary_mantel" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a>, <a href="https://plus.google.com/+HilarymantelAuthor/posts" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Google +</a> and <a href="https://www.facebook.com/HilaryMantelAuthor" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">FB</a> pages<br><br>Excellent <a href="http://www.wnyc.org/story/287382-what-were-reading-now-bring-bodies-hilary-mantel/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">radio interview</a> with Mantel by Leonard Lopate<br><br>A marvelous New Yorker magazine article looking at <a href="http://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2012/10/15/the-dead-are-real" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> Mantel’s career</a><br><br>Great material here in another New Yorker article, <a href="http://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2012/05/07/invitation-to-a-beheading" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Invitation to a Beheading</a>, by James Wood<br><br><a href="http://www.telegraph.co.uk/culture/tvandradio/11360747/Wolf-Hall-explained.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> Why isn't Henry VIII fat and other Wolf Hall mysteries explained<br></a></span>

<div id="review_409835337" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/409835337" />
    <a title="Fionnuala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala"><img alt="Fionnuala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368182051p2/5498525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/409835337?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fionnuala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fionnuala" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007315093" href="/book/show/13393094-bring-up-the-bodies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=21st-century-classics">21st-century-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer409835337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5684561012022554988">Most people in the English speaking world know the history of Henry VIII from their earliest school days or from the many books, films and TV series that the episode has inspired. Some of us cringe when we hear of yet another fictional version, yet another glittery effort to sensationalise the intrigue of the Tudor court and create even more farfetched scenarios around the details of the wooing and discarding of Henry’s wives. How then can Hilary Mantel’s series be of any interest? I would argue</span>
  <span id="freeText5684561012022554988" style="display:none">Most people in the English speaking world know the history of Henry VIII from their earliest school days or from the many books, films and TV series that the episode has inspired. Some of us cringe when we hear of yet another fictional version, yet another glittery effort to sensationalise the intrigue of the Tudor court and create even more farfetched scenarios around the details of the wooing and discarding of Henry’s wives. How then can Hilary Mantel’s series be of any interest? I would argue that her treatment of history does not belong in the historical fiction genre but rather in the field of historical analysis. Already, with her account of the French Revolution, <i>A Place of Greater Safety</i> (2006), she proved that she had found a new and unique way to analyse historical events. She combs all the available documentary evidence and from this wealth of detail she builds a living picture of the time and its chief players so that we feel, smell, touch, even inhabit their world. <br />But she creates dialogue, you will point out, and imagines the players’ thoughts. This is true, but any historian who reads all the documents, all of the letters relating to historical personages can’t avoid interpreting their motivations. Mantel takes that interpretation a step further through dialogue, thereby making the reading of this familiar history, which otherwise holds little surprise or suspense, much more rewarding and entertaining. She succeeds because of her fine writing skills and her ability to choose the most suitable angle from which to view the events, in this case, Thomas Cromwell, the kings secretary; it is as if the reader is perched on his shoulder, seeing everything he sees and privy to about half of his thoughts. And we suspect that the other half of his thoughts are so secret that even he, Thomas Cromwell has little access to them. Mantel reveals him as neither hero nor villain, but simply a man who is good at his job. Being Henry’s secretary is like playing chess; the task is to destroy the opponent and the notion of the opposition of good and evil has no place on this board. Survival is the only rule. Let me give an example: on the first page of <i>Bring Up the Bodies</i>, Cromwell watches a hunting scene involving a pair of hawks: <br /><br />	<i>“Weightless, they glide on the upper currents of air. They pity no one. They answer to no one. Their lives are simple. When they look down they see nothing but their prey, and the borrowed plumes of the hunters: they see a flittering, flinching universe, a universe filled with their dinner. All summer has been like this, a riot of dismemberment, fur and feather flying..."</i><br /><br />When we reach the last page of the book, we remember the pair of hawks and we are amazed. Thomas Cromwell has learned from those hawks. He too is silent when he takes his prey.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5684561012022554988" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_436076737" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/436076737" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/436076737?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Annet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Annet" href="/user/show/1166369-annet">Annet</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007315109" href="/book/show/17899680-bring-up-the-bodies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166369-annet?shelf=wow-impressive">wow-impressive</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166369-annet?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166369-annet?shelf=very-british">very-british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166369-annet?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166369-annet?shelf=brilliant">brilliant</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166369-annet?shelf=family-ties">family-ties</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166369-annet?shelf=dark">dark</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166369-annet?shelf=beautiful-poetic">beautiful-poetic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer436076737" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13552632270040898037"><i>There are no endings. If you think so you are deceived as to their nature. They are all beginnings. Here is one....</i><br/><br/>The books of Hilary Mantel on Thomas Cromwell are superb, grande. We all know the history of Henry VIII but Wolfhall and Bring Up the Bodies are refreshing, sharp, intelligent, emotional...so much more than 'just' historic tales. I give a slight preference to Wolf Hall, because that book was groundbreaking, a new take on this famous piece of history, seen through the eyes of Thomas </span>
  <span id="freeText13552632270040898037" style="display:none"><i>There are no endings. If you think so you are deceived as to their nature. They are all beginnings. Here is one....</i><br /><br />The books of Hilary Mantel on Thomas Cromwell are superb, grande. We all know the history of Henry VIII but Wolfhall and Bring Up the Bodies are refreshing, sharp, intelligent, emotional...so much more than 'just' historic tales. I give a slight preference to Wolf Hall, because that book was groundbreaking, a new take on this famous piece of history, seen through the eyes of Thomas Cromwell. However, again Bring up the bodies is sublime and of course tells the dark tale of the fall of Anne Boleyn, fascinating. And Mantel is a great storyteller...<br /><br /><i>Something happens to Anne then, which later he will not quite understand. She seems to dissolve and slip from their grasp, from Kingston's hands and his, she seems to liquefy and elude them, and when she resolves herself once more into woman's form she is on hands and knees on the cobbles, her head thrown back, wailing. <br />Fitzwilliam, the Lord Chancellor, even her uncle, steps back; Kingston frowns, his deputy shakes his head, Richard Riche looks stricken. He, Cromwell, takes hold of her - since no one else will do it - and sets her back on her feet. She weighs nothing, and as he lifts her, her wail breaks off, as if her breath had been stopped. Silent, she steadies herself against his shoulder, leans into him: intent, complicit, read for the next thing they will do together, which is kill her.</i><br /><br />About Cromwell.... what a fascinating character, re. my review of Wolf Hall, on the one hand a warm family man, on the other hand iron-hard when it comes to dealing with all the so-called 'accomplices' in the Anne Boleyn case and ruthlessly sending a whole group of people to their death by axe... Revenge... or self preservation? or both.... One thing is for sure: Cromwell is always planning ahead. A true chess player. And so far, he does that pretty well. Also very interesting in this book, the developing relation/friendship with Chapuys, ambassador of emperor Charles V. <br /><br /><i>'All the players are gone', Wriothesley says. 'All four who carried the cardinal to Hell. And also the poor foul Mark who made a ballad of their exploits.' 'All four, he says. 'All five.' 'A gentleman asked me, if this is what Cromwell does to the cardinal's lesser enemies, what will he do by and by to the King himself?</i><br /><br />I am now watching the dvd of the BBC series that I was very careful not to touch while I was still reading the book... Very good as well. The part on Thomas More stands out in the series so far... for me. <br />Highly recommended. I do hope Ms. Mantel continues the Cromwell / Henry VIII story until the very end.... Can't wait for the third book. <br /><br /><br />&lt;</span>

    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/341420454?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer341420454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11744021599440844287">I came to this sequel thinking it could not possibly stand up to the first installment. So, I was prepared to like this book, but not love it as much as I did <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6520929.Wolf_Hall' title='Wolf Hall by Hilary Mantel' rel='nofollow noopener'>Wolf Hall</a>. But I was wrong: it does, and I did. <br/><br/>It's one of those works that I lingered over the last pages of, not wanting it to end: the prose is that good. And it installed itself into my psyche. After putting it down at night and as I fell asleep, words, phrases, sentences rolled through my head. (This has happened to me before, but th</span>
  <span id="freeText11744021599440844287" style="display:none">I came to this sequel thinking it could not possibly stand up to the first installment. So, I was prepared to like this book, but not love it as much as I did <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6520929.Wolf_Hall" title="Wolf Hall by Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolf Hall</a>. But I was wrong: it does, and I did. <br /><br />It's one of those works that I lingered over the last pages of, not wanting it to end: the prose is that good. And it installed itself into my psyche. After putting it down at night and as I fell asleep, words, phrases, sentences rolled through my head. (This has happened to me before, but this time it felt different.) And though when I awoke, I couldn't remember any of what I'd dreamed (if dreaming is what it was), I knew the procession of words was due to this book. I also figured this is how the brain of Mantel's Cromwell must work, never stopping, except he does remember all. And when you see the culmination of his remembering all, it is chilling.<br /><br />Much of what I wrote in my review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6520929.Wolf_Hall" title="Wolf Hall by Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolf Hall</a> may be inserted here. Like the title of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6520929.Wolf_Hall" title="Wolf Hall by Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolf Hall</a>, this title has a different meaning than you might think (unless you are exceptionally in-the-know). And as I also said about <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6520929.Wolf_Hall" title="Wolf Hall by Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolf Hall</a>, this is not your average, run-of-the-mill historical fiction: it is elevated. <br /><br />As I neared the end, I was starting to become resigned to the fact that I wouldn't be as excited by any particular passage as I had been with the one I quoted in my review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6520929.Wolf_Hall" title="Wolf Hall by Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolf Hall</a> (and that perhaps I was spoiled by what was so fresh in "Wolf Hall") but then I arrived at the final page ...</span>
  <a data-text-id="11744021599440844287" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3193612954" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3193612954" />
    <a title="Henk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76202320-henk"><img alt="Henk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557871304p2/76202320.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3193612954?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henk" href="/user/show/76202320-henk">Henk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789056724948" href="/book/show/20486455-het-boek-henry">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76202320-henk?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76202320-henk?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3193612954" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6075942892359717464"><b>Much more fast paced and focused than it’s predecessor, feeling effortless and thrilling at the same time - 5 stars</b><br/><i>You can be merry with the king, you can share a joke with him. But as Thomas More used to say, it's like sporting with a tamed lion. You tousle its mane and pull its ears, but all the time you're thinking, those claws, those claws, those claws.</i><br/><br/>Sometimes I needed to press myself to read on in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7826803.Wolf_Hall__Thomas_Cromwell___1_' title='Wolf Hall (Thomas Cromwell, #1) by Hilary Mantel' rel='nofollow noopener'>Wolf Hall</a>; with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13507212.Bring_Up_the_Bodies__Thomas_Cromwell___2_' title='Bring Up the Bodies (Thomas Cromwell, #2) by Hilary Mantel' rel='nofollow noopener'>Bring Up the Bodies</a> I stayed up late just to keep on reading. A breathless j</span>
  <span id="freeText6075942892359717464" style="display:none"><b>Much more fast paced and focused than it’s predecessor, feeling effortless and thrilling at the same time - 5 stars</b><br /><i>You can be merry with the king, you can share a joke with him. But as Thomas More used to say, it's like sporting with a tamed lion. You tousle its mane and pull its ears, but all the time you're thinking, those claws, those claws, those claws.</i><br /><br />Sometimes I needed to press myself to read on in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7826803.Wolf_Hall__Thomas_Cromwell___1_" title="Wolf Hall (Thomas Cromwell, #1) by Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">Wolf Hall</a>; with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13507212.Bring_Up_the_Bodies__Thomas_Cromwell___2_" title="Bring Up the Bodies (Thomas Cromwell, #2) by Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">Bring Up the Bodies</a> I stayed up late just to keep on reading. A breathless joy of a drama to read, with us watching over Cromwell’s shoulder every step of the way.<br /><br /><b>Long live the king and his first advisor...</b><br /><i>But Henry had set up: ‘I can do as it pleases me,’ his monarch said. ‘God would not allow my pleasure to be contrary to his design, nor my designs to be impeded by his will.’ A shadow of cunning had crossed his face.</i><br /><br />In Dutch the title of this second installment is The Book Henry and I think that is quite fitting, since everything revolves around him. <br />Cromwell thinks to himself that his only skill is interpreting and predicting the whims of the king, and daydreams of writing a book on this topic to his son. Also when Henry is hurt in an accident we get to see how much of the power dynamics flow directly from him as absolutist monarch.<br /><br />Cromwell shows some rare emotion when a duke tells him to stay out of the affairs of his superiors. It does make you wonder why he did not elevate himself into nobility and a bit more stable footing sooner, something the Seymours definitely don’t have any qualms about when their daughter gets into the favour of the king. He is still the pragmatic fixer of Wolf Hall, but can, due to his reputation and clear favour of the king, now rely a bit more on words and suggestion than real life confrontations (<i>I cherish diplomacy. It’s cheap</i>). Towards the end of the novel we also see the fruition of a very long in the making revenge for his first protector Cardinal Wolsey, showing some of his inner life and the abacus of his conscience. Also somewhere one thirds into the book we have <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/58851.Hilary_Mantel" title="Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">Hilary Mantel</a> letting Cromwell think slightly anachronistically about what he wants to achieve for England and the average citizen, raising their living conditions to cement the power of the Tudor monarchy:<br /><i>It is better not to try people, not to force them to desperation. Make them prosper; out of superfluity they will be generous. Full bellies breed gentle manners. The pinch of famine makes monsters.</i><br /><br />What helps him at court is that he can now rely on his meritocratic entourage as a remedy versus the various warring factions; sometimes he felt like a chess player wielding his pieces.<br />Supporters he needs, when most of his attention is drawn to the showdown with first Catherine of Aragon and then Anne Boleyn.<br /><br /><b>... while the queens die conveniently, either natural or by a French executioner</b><br /><i>The queen is plotting something, I know not what, something devious, something dark, perhaps so dark that she herself does not know what it is, and as yet us only dreaming of it: but I must be quick, I must dream it for her, I shall dream it into being.</i><br /><br />Anne shows the predicament of women in her day and age. When they can’t bring an heir they are discarded and they don’t have power from themselves. One of her ladies in waiting inspires Cromwell to think the following: <i>But a young married gentlewoman has no way to help herself. She has no more power than a donkey; all she can hope for is a master who spares the whip.</i><br />But the same lady in waiting aids in the downfall of the queen, who has not made herself popular by her sharpness (<i>She opens her lips and out slides the devil’s tail.</i>). A whole <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13496.A_Game_of_Thrones__A_Song_of_Ice_and_Fire___1_" title="A Game of Thrones (A Song of Ice and Fire, #1) by George R.R. Martin" rel="nofollow noopener">A Game of Thrones</a> incest plot is brought against her and we have Henry talking like Donald Trump about Anne (with her just being "too evil") after her fall.<br /><br /><b>Truth is singular. Its "versions" are mistruths - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6538289.David_Mitchell" title="David Mitchell" rel="nofollow noopener">David Mitchell</a> in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/951170.Cloud_Atlas" title="Cloud Atlas by David Mitchell" rel="nofollow noopener">Cloud Atlas</a></b><br /><i>What is the nature of the border between truth and lies? It is permeable and blurred because it is planted thick with rumour, confabulation, misunderstandings and twisted tales. Truth can break the gates down, truth can howl in the street; unless truth is pleasing, personable and easy to like, she is condemned to stay whimpering at the back door.</i><br /><br />A lot of the book, and the best quotes in it, are about the nature of justice and truth. <br />Law and judgement are used as an instrument of royal will instead of truth finding (reminiscent of the whole impeachment “trial” we saw only so very recently) by Henry and Cromwell.<br /><br />Even Thomas Cromwell's own son raises questions concerning this: <i>When Gregory says, ‘Are they guilty?’ he means, ‘Did they do it?’ But when he says, ‘Are they guilty?’ he means, ‘Did the court find them so?’ The lawyer’s world is entire unto itself, the human pared away.</i><br />Or a bit more admiringly: <i>I see. It is not so much, who is guilty, as whose guilt is of service to you</i> from Thomas Wriothesley.<br />Finally Cromwell even thinks to himself:<i>He needs guilty men. So he has found men who are guilty. Though perhaps not guilty as charged</i>, showing some consciousness about the things he need to do for his king.<br /><br />I left Cromwell and Bring Up The Bodies at the beheading of Anne Boleyn, being totally in awe and feeling very fortunate that the release of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45992717.The_Mirror___the_Light__Thomas_Cromwell___3_" title="The Mirror &amp; the Light (Thomas Cromwell, #3) by Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">The Mirror &amp; the Light</a> is only a few weeks away. <br />Can't wait to see how Hilary Mantel, no doubt brilliantly, manages to finish this trilogy!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6075942892359717464" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12582808026277867086">Aaaahhh. Fine, fine, fine. The final last paragraph -- perfect.<br/>Cromwell now to me will always be "he, Cromwell". This little stylistic flourish did add clarity, compared with Wolf Hall. To purposefully use just "he" in the first book was at times confusing, forcing one to stop and step out of the story to regain one's bearings. Sort of like breaking the fourth wall -- and perhaps that was the point then, a metafictional technique? but it was too intrusive. <br/>This book just sailed on from Wolf Hall</span>
  <span id="freeText12582808026277867086" style="display:none">Aaaahhh. Fine, fine, fine. The final last paragraph -- perfect.<br />Cromwell now to me will always be "he, Cromwell". This little stylistic flourish did add clarity, compared with Wolf Hall. To purposefully use just "he" in the first book was at times confusing, forcing one to stop and step out of the story to regain one's bearings. Sort of like breaking the fourth wall -- and perhaps that was the point then, a metafictional technique? but it was too intrusive. <br />This book just sailed on from Wolf Hall. Immersion was swift and total.<br />Another Booker winner, I hope.</span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/323791510?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer323791510" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14696804159365984103">I loved this second book about Thomas Cromwell and King Henry VIII even more than the first one!<br/><br/>I started reading <i>Bring Up the Bodies</i> as soon as I finished <i>Wolf Hall</i>, and I've enjoyed this series so much I'm excited for Mantel's third volume, whenever it's published. <br/><br/>While <i>Wolf Hall</i> focused on the rise of Anne Boleyn and how she became Queen of England, <i>Bring Up the Bodies</i> is about how the King decides to leave Anne when she can't give him a son, and her subsequent downfall and execution. The st</span>
  <span id="freeText14696804159365984103" style="display:none">I loved this second book about Thomas Cromwell and King Henry VIII even more than the first one!<br /><br />I started reading <i>Bring Up the Bodies</i> as soon as I finished <i>Wolf Hall</i>, and I've enjoyed this series so much I'm excited for Mantel's third volume, whenever it's published. <br /><br />While <i>Wolf Hall</i> focused on the rise of Anne Boleyn and how she became Queen of England, <i>Bring Up the Bodies</i> is about how the King decides to leave Anne when she can't give him a son, and her subsequent downfall and execution. The story of her trial and beheading has been told many times, but I loved how Mantel chose to show us the scenes from Cromwell's perspective, and how he helped manipulate the proceedings. Cromwell even maneuvered to help the King find his next wife, Jane Seymour.<br /><br />This second book had good pacing and flowed more freely than the first one, perhaps because the first one had numerous flashbacks to Cromwell's childhood and the back-and-forth with Cardinal Wolsey. Taken together, they are a masterpiece of historical fiction, and I highly recommend this series. <br /><br /><b>Favorite Quotes</b><br />"You can be merry with the king, you can share a joke with him. But as Thomas More used to say, it's like sporting with a tamed lion. You tousle its mane and pull its ears, but all the time you're thinking, those claws, those claws, those claws."<br /><br />"What is the nature of the border between truth and lies? It is permeable and blurred because it is planted thick with rumour, confabulation, misunderstandings and twisted tales. Truth can break the gates down, truth can howl in the street; unless truth is pleasing, personable and easy to like, she is condemned to stay whimpering at the back door."<br /><br />"He needs guilty men. So he has found men who are guilty. Though perhaps not guilty as charged." <br /><br />"Sometimes peace looks like war, you cannot tell them apart."<br /><br />"Erasmus says that you should praise a ruler even for qualities he does not have. For the flattery gives him to think. And the qualities he presently lacks, he might go to work on them." <br /><br />"How many men can say, as I must, 'I am a man whose only friend is the King of England'? I have everything, you would think. And yet take Henry away, and I have nothing." <br /><br />"We are not priests. We don’t want their sort of confession. We are lawyers. We want the truth little by little and only those parts of it we can use."<br /><br />"You have always regarded women as disposable, my lord, and you cannot complain if in the end they think the same of you." <br /><br />"Who can understand the lives of women?"</span>
  <a data-text-id="14696804159365984103" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer18285567035665706758">⭐️⭐️⭐️⭐️⭐️ Historical fiction at its best, <b>Bring up the Bodies</b> is a worthy winner of the Man Booker Prize. It is well researched and very well written. Once I re-attuned myself to the extensive use of the pronoun ‘he’ referring to Thomas Cromwell, it was a most pleasurable read. The subtle humour and wily Cromwell’s wit were particularly enjoyable, and I look forward to reading <b>The Mirror and the Light</b>, the recently published final instalment of this trilogy.<br/><br/><blockquote><i>“This book is of course not about Ann</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText18285567035665706758" style="display:none">⭐️⭐️⭐️⭐️⭐️ Historical fiction at its best, <b>Bring up the Bodies</b> is a worthy winner of the Man Booker Prize. It is well researched and very well written. Once I re-attuned myself to the extensive use of the pronoun ‘he’ referring to Thomas Cromwell, it was a most pleasurable read. The subtle humour and wily Cromwell’s wit were particularly enjoyable, and I look forward to reading <b>The Mirror and the Light</b>, the recently published final instalment of this trilogy.<br /><br /><blockquote><i>“This book is of course not about Anne Boleyn or about Henry VIII, but about the career of Thomas Cromwell, who is still in need of attention from biographers. Meanwhile, Mr Secretary remains sleek, plump and densely inaccessible, like a choice plum in a Christmas pie; but I hope to continue my efforts to dig him out.”</i> [Hilary Mantel - Author’s Note, Bring up the Bodies]</blockquote></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9449422088002178366">Bring Up the Bodies (Thomas Cromwell, #2), Hilary Mantel <br/><br/>Bring Up the Bodies is a historical novel by Hilary Mantel and sequel to her award-winning Wolf Hall. It is the second part of a planned trilogy charting the rise and fall of Thomas Cromwell, the powerful minister in the court of King Henry VIII. It is to be followed by The Mirror and the Light. <br/><br/><br/>Bring Up the Bodies begins not long after the conclusion of Wolf Hall. The King and Thomas Cromwell, who is now Master Secretary to the King's Pri</span>
  <span id="freeText9449422088002178366" style="display:none">Bring Up the Bodies (Thomas Cromwell, #2), Hilary Mantel <br /><br />Bring Up the Bodies is a historical novel by Hilary Mantel and sequel to her award-winning Wolf Hall. It is the second part of a planned trilogy charting the rise and fall of Thomas Cromwell, the powerful minister in the court of King Henry VIII. It is to be followed by The Mirror and the Light. <br /><br /><br />Bring Up the Bodies begins not long after the conclusion of Wolf Hall. The King and Thomas Cromwell, who is now Master Secretary to the King's Privy Council, are the guests of the Seymour family at their manor house, Wolf Hall. The King shares private moments with Jane Seymour, and begins to fall in love with her. <br /><br />His present queen, Anne Boleyn, has failed to give him a male heir. Their relationship is a stormy one, sometimes loving and sometimes characterized by angry quarrels. At length, the King tells Cromwell privately, "I cannot live as I have." <br /><br />Cromwell understands this to mean that the King has tired of a wife who gives him neither peace nor a son and wants his marriage to her ended. Cromwell promises the King he will find a legal way to make this happen.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و یکم ماه ژوئن سال 2014میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: مجرمان را بیاورید - کتاب دوم؛ نویسنده: هیلاری منتل؛ مترجم: علی اکبر قاضی زاده؛ تهران، تندیس، 1392؛ در 543ص؛ شابک 9786001821011؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان انگلیسی سده 21م<br /><br />داستانی تاریخی اثر نویسنده انگلیسی، «هیلاری منتل» است، که نخستین بار در سال 2012میلادی منتشر شد؛ این کتاب که دومین جلد از سه‌ گانه «هیلاری منتل» درباره ی قدرت گرفتن و سقوط «تامس کرامول» است<br /><br />رخدادهای این کتاب که در فاصله تابستان سال 1535میلادی تا تابستان سال 1536میلادی رخ می‌دهند، روایتی داستانی از آغاز علاقه «هنری هشتم» به «جین سیمور»، متهم شدن «آن بولین» به زنا، و نهایتاً اعدام وی، و نقش «کرامول» در این رخدادهاست؛ جلد نخست این مجموعه با عنوان «تالار گرگ» منتشر شده، و جلد سوم از سه‌ گانه «آینه و نور» نام دارد، که تاکنون در ایران منتشر نشده است<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 07/07/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

    <a title="Melindam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36434323-melindam"><img alt="Melindam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484595601p2/36434323.jpg" /></a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9780007467884" href="/book/show/43091307-bring-up-the-bodies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36434323-melindam?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36434323-melindam?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36434323-melindam?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3205849260" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13600208011099200341"><i>“Rafe asks him, could the king's freedom be obtained, sir, with more economy of means? Less bloodshed?<br/>Look, he says: once you have exhausted the process of negotiation and compromise, one you have fixed on the destruction of an enemy, that destruction must be swift and it must be perfect. Before you even glance in his direction, you should have his name on a warrant, the ports blocked, his wife and friends bought, his heir under your protection, his money in your strong room and his dog running </i></span>
  <span id="freeText13600208011099200341" style="display:none"><i>“Rafe asks him, could the king's freedom be obtained, sir, with more economy of means? Less bloodshed?<br>Look, he says: once you have exhausted the process of negotiation and compromise, one you have fixed on the destruction of an enemy, that destruction must be swift and it must be perfect. Before you even glance in his direction, you should have his name on a warrant, the ports blocked, his wife and friends bought, his heir under your protection, his money in your strong room and his dog running to your whistle. Before he wakes in the morning, you should have the axe in your hand.”<br><br>“Your love of glory must conquer your will to survive; or why fight at all? Why not be a smith, a brewer, a wool merchant? Why are you in the contest, if not to win, and if not to win, then to die?”</i><br><br><b>Meet again Thomas Cromwell, puppet-master, self-made man &amp; Renaissance Godfather.<br>He is THE trouble-shooter &amp; fixer for Henry VIII and Tudor Celebrities whether it comes to marriage, politics, economy, religion or anything else they require. </b><br>You want the Bible translated into English? Or dissolve the monasteries to get money? - Cromwell is your man.<br>You want another divorce? With clear conscience? No problem, if Cromwell is on your side... <br><br>The more I think about him, the more he, as a character, reminds me of Vito Corleone from the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22034.The_Godfather" title="The Godfather by Mario Puzo" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1394988109l/22034._SY75_.jpg" alt="The Godfather by Mario Puzo" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a>.<br><br>As Don Corleone said: <i>“The lawyer with the briefcase can steal more money than the man with the gun.” </i> And <i>“Never hate your enemies. It affects your judgment.”</i> As well as <i> “Great men are not born great, they grow great . . .”</i> <br>And doesn't Cromwell KNOW ALL THAT! :)<br><br>Mantel's story is amazing, going from strength to strength. No 2nd-book syndrome here, for sure. Thomas Cromwell continues to fascinate me. There is something in Hilary Mantel's style that makes these novels read more like magical realism than historical fiction, they have the power to bewitch and to suck you in their world completely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13600208011099200341" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <a title="Heidi The Reader" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heidi The Reader" href="/user/show/7986781-heidi-the-reader">Heidi The Reader</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7986781-heidi-the-reader?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7986781-heidi-the-reader?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7986781-heidi-the-reader?shelf=the-numinous-book-club">the-numinous-book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7986781-heidi-the-reader?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2402894855" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1237446442164047263">Hilary Mantel's brilliant trilogy about Thomas Cromwell continues with <i>Bring Up the Bodies</i>.<br/><br/>Cromwell is the right-hand man of Henry the VIII. His masterful manipulation of people and circumstances to make the world as Henry wants it has brought Cromwell wealth and power.<br/><br/>Getting Anne Boleyn on the throne was a struggle. Now he has to get her off of it without losing his own head in the process.<br/><br/>Mantel doesn't just tell history, she makes it come alive.<br/><br/>In one scene I can't get out of my head: Henry </span>
  <span id="freeText1237446442164047263" style="display:none">Hilary Mantel's brilliant trilogy about Thomas Cromwell continues with <i>Bring Up the Bodies</i>.<br /><br />Cromwell is the right-hand man of Henry the VIII. His masterful manipulation of people and circumstances to make the world as Henry wants it has brought Cromwell wealth and power.<br /><br />Getting Anne Boleyn on the throne was a struggle. Now he has to get her off of it without losing his own head in the process.<br /><br />Mantel doesn't just tell history, she makes it come alive.<br /><br />In one scene I can't get out of my head: Henry has a temper tantrum because of the Spanish ambassador's continued disrespect towards his new wife, Anne, and the repeated requests from the Spanish crown for money owed. The king blows his top at Cromwell and screams in his face.<br /><br />He says he believes Cromwell has always manipulated him and laughed at him. But he is king and he will not be steered.<br /><br />And, even though I knew the history, I thought for a moment Cromwell was going to be taken to the Tower in that instant.<br /><br />Instead, he quietly apologizes to the king and dismisses himself, then goes to a different room to take a drink. With shaking hands, Cromwell spills a drop of the wine on himself and sits there, contemplating the small stain on his shirt.<br /><br />And I said to myself, "Mantel is a genius."<br /><br />In that passage, it was as if I was in that room, living the moment. She makes you forget you're reading a book. It's so immersive. It's almost magical.<br /><br />Cromwell's efforts to collect evidence against Queen Anne fills much of this book. As he tightens his net around her, you can almost feel it tighten around yourself.<br /><br />Cromwell jokes with his sworn men to ease some of the tension, but it is always there, buzzing beneath the surface.<br /><br />Highly recommended for historical fiction readers. <i>Bring Up the Bodies</i> is one of the best books I've read this year.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1237446442164047263" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1543028686" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1543028686" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51395417-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506113266p2/51395417.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1543028686?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/51395417-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1543028686" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer469199566735459502">In "Bring up the Bodies" Hilary Mantel has written a shorter and tighter novel than its predecessor "Wolf Hall", and it is just as good! I tore through the book in a few days, and I am eagerly anticipating the third and final installment in the series.<br/>Most of the joy of "Bring up the Bodies" is Mantel's lovely writing, and her masterful creation and depiction of the series' main protagonist Thomas Cromwell. The story is told mainly from a third person perspective, but it is an omniscient narrato</span>
  <span id="freeText469199566735459502" style="display:none">In "Bring up the Bodies" Hilary Mantel has written a shorter and tighter novel than its predecessor "Wolf Hall", and it is just as good! I tore through the book in a few days, and I am eagerly anticipating the third and final installment in the series.<br />Most of the joy of "Bring up the Bodies" is Mantel's lovely writing, and her masterful creation and depiction of the series' main protagonist Thomas Cromwell. The story is told mainly from a third person perspective, but it is an omniscient narrator who sometimes enters the minds and thoughts of the characters, so we occasionally get a first person point of view. This stylistic choice further adds to the novel's strengths as a well written text.<br />As already mentioned, one of the main pleasures of this book is Mantel's creation of Cromwell, a historical figure we actually don't know a lot about. Her interpretation of this man is wonderful and full bodied on so many levels. Cromwell's dry humor and accurate/insightful observations about others and our human natures are one of the book's main joys. He is an intellectual giant among worms, and he enjoys himself immensely. His almost perverse pleasure at ensnaring into the web of Anne Boleyn's downfall the four noblemen who mocked his late beloved mentor Cardinal Wolsey in a play years before is an excellent plot contrivance that Mantel's creates to show that Cromwell is capable of pettiness, and is also quite dangerous. Mantel deploys considerable skill in the four scenes where Cromwell entraps each of these four men into his web. He is the perfect Machiavellian, doing a service for his king, and himself at the same time. Another breathtaking moment in the text (among many) is the excellent scene where Cromwell tricks Mark Smeaton (a dandy musician) into "confessing" adultery with Queen Anne. It is simply riveting writing.<br />In "Bring up the Bodies" Mantel shows Cromwell growing into his power, and thus he is not nearly as likable as he was in "Wolf Hall". He is becoming a lot like the people he detests. Mantel is obviously building up his ego and hubris for his downfall, which I assume is the focus of the third/ final book. There are so many ironic and foreshadowing lines that hint at Cromwell's own end that I kept thinking as I read, "you reap what you sow". If you know the actual history of these events you will catch these references and it will increase your enjoyment of the book. Pride is such a huge theme in this text, and it trips up so many people that one would be a dunce not to see the larger warnings about us as individuals that we can take from the novel.<br />In short, Mantel's writing is fluid and lyrical and she is the rare storyteller who is also an excellent writer. Although "Bring up the Bodies" is less episodic and quicker to read than "Wolf Hall" it is no less enjoyable, and I can't wait for the next one!</span>
  <a data-text-id="469199566735459502" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
